Summarizing the trend analysis results of Manufacturing AI Transformation (AX).

This analysis aims to identify the latest trends in manufacturing AI technology and derive key application areas and challenges. As a result of the analysis, AI is driving innovation, particularly in the fields of quality inspection and process optimization. Real-time defect detection in 3D printing and welding processes utilizing computer vision, and closed-loop technology that immediately reflects these detections in control, emerged as key trends. Furthermore, AI is expanding beyond the production floor into enterprise-wide management areas, such as automating quotation and production scheduling by combining with ERP systems, and enhancing cybersecurity in IT/OT convergence environments.

This trend provides an opportunity for the realization of 'Intelligent Autonomous Factories' that maximize productivity and quality. However, for successful AI adoption, the challenges of establishing clear data governance and resolving data silos between departments must be addressed first. Therefore, carefully planning pilot projects that can create real value and enhancing data readiness will be key to success.

This paper proposes a closed-loop quality management architecture combining camera+Raspberry Pi+OctoPrint-based real-time vision (optical/thermal imaging), statistical process control such as EWMA, and models like CNN and SVM in FDM/FFF (and metal LPBF) additive manufacturing. Upon field implementation, defect detection accuracy of 84-99% (e.g., inter-layer delamination 97.8%) and immediate feedback can be expected. It organizes defect classification, such as warping and delamination, and the causal relationships of parameters including layer thickness, raster angle/width, nozzle, bed temperature, speed, and infill, thereby providing a smart factory-type AM quality assurance roadmap that includes automatic parameter readjustment (pause, resume, slicing update). Additionally, by including layer-by-layer data collection, labeling, synthetic data utilization, and an MLOps retraining guide, it provides practical assistance in reducing scrap/rework, energy, and raw material, as well as multi-printer operation, and strengthening certification and traceability.

This review systematically organizes the latest trends and implementation methods of AI-augmented closed-loop 3D printing. Focusing on FDM/FFF, it presents defect types and causes, the influence of process parameters, layer-by-layer vision inspection and thermal imaging-based real-time detection, model performance examples (e.g., warping in the 99% range) using CNN/SVM, and closed-loop architectures linked with OctoPrint and Raspberry Pi, thereby enhancing practical applicability. It provides practical perspectives from data collection, labeling, and synthetic data to MLOps, and demonstrates the possibility of expansion to metal LPBF and the effects of quality, cost, and delivery time improvement in multi-printer operations. From the perspective of Manufacturing AX, it is a high-value reference material that can be immediately utilized for AM quality automation, scrap reduction, autonomous parameter optimization, and strengthening certification response.

Multimodal AI (MaVila) implements closed-loop quality control that explains defects in inline vision inspection and immediately adjusts equipment parameters, and can be quickly adopted by small and medium-sized manufacturers through low-data learning. Combining OPC UA/MQTT integration, safety interlocks and change management, data and inference log governance, and HPC/digital twin-based rapid simulation can accelerate the speed from PoC to pilot to rollout and improve key indicators such as PPk/CPk, and scrap/rework rates.

CSUN's MaVila aims for closed-loop quality control by combining vision and natural language to identify and describe defects, and by communicating with equipment in real-time to adjust parameters. It can achieve performance even with small amounts of training data, which can lower adoption barriers for small and medium-sized manufacturers, and with NRP/HPC support, simulation and verification speeds can be increased. Initially, a phased approach is effective, starting with adding explanations and guidance to inline vision inspection and expanding to limited automatic adjustment, including safety guardrails and approval procedures. When implementing, it is key to concurrently build OPC UA/MQTT connectivity, safety interlocks, change management, and data/prompt log management systems, and to manage performance with KPIs such as improvements in false positive/negative rates, cycle time, and PPk/CPk.

In manufacturing, cybersecurity has emerged as a key external risk, second only to economic conditions, and AI/ML-based security is becoming a top priority in smart factory and AX roadmaps, with 61% of security personnel planning to adopt AI. To reduce the expanded attack surface due to IT/OT convergence, secure OT asset visibility, apply ISA/IEC 62443-based network segmentation, least privilege, and MFA, and gradually increase automation linked with SIEM/SOAR by utilizing AI for ICS traffic anomaly detection, UEBA, and alarm noise reduction. Simultaneously, by promoting quick-win tasks within 90 days, such as NIST CSF/IEC 62443 compliance checks, establishing baselines through passive monitoring, and backup/recovery rehearsals, you can improve both security maturity (including MTTD/MTTR) and uptime.

This article presents a practical framework for accelerating DFM/DFY and yield improvement in semiconductor manufacturing by integrating design (EDA) layout and fab process and metrology data with machine learning, and provides specific use cases such as lithography hotspot detection, virtual metrology-based CD/film thickness prediction, FDC anomaly detection and predictive maintenance, and test/die binning optimization. In terms of implementation, it emphasizes combining layout, recipe, tool, and environmental contexts, SECS/GEM and E142-based data governance and IP protection, MLOps, Human-in-the-loop (HITL), and periodic retraining. It also guides the integration of challenges related to 3D stacking/packaging and power/thermal management, arising from the proliferation of AI/HPC, into the DTCO/STCO roadmap.

í†µí•© ì¸ì‚¬ì´íŠ¸:Consolidated Insight:
ì—£ì§€ ì»´í“¨íŒ…, ê°•í™”í•™ìŠµ, ë””ì§€í„¸ íŠ¸ìœˆì„ ê²°í•©í•´ ë¡œë´‡ ê¸°ë°˜ ì‹¤ì‹œê°„ ê³µì •ì„ ì €ì§€ì—°Â·ê³ ìœ ì—°ì„±ìœ¼ë¡œ ìš´ì˜í•˜ëŠ” ì‹¤í–‰ í”„ë ˆì„ì„ ì œì‹œí•œë‹¤. íŒŒì¼ëŸ¿ ì…€ì—ì„œ ì¹´ë©”ë¼/í† í¬ì„¼ì„œë¥¼ ë¡œì»¬ ì²˜ë¦¬(<20ms), ì‹œë®¬ë ˆì´í„° ì‚¬ì „í•™ìŠµê³¼ sim-to-real ì ì‘(ISO 10218/TS 15066 ì¤€ìˆ˜), ë””ì§€í„¸ íŠ¸ìœˆ ê¸°ë°˜ ê°€ìƒ ì»¤ë¯¸ì…”ë‹ê³¼ MES/PLC ë™ê¸°í™”ë¡œ ì‹œì‘í•˜ë¼. ë¹„ì „ í”½ì•¤í”Œë ˆì´ìŠ¤Â·AMR ê²½ë¡œ ìµœì í™”Â·ì‹¤ì‹œê°„ í’ˆì§ˆê²€ì‚¬ë¥¼ ìš°ì„  ì ìš©í•˜ê³ , ì‚¬ì´í´íƒ€ì„Â·OEEÂ·ë¶ˆëŸ‰ë¥ Â·ë³€ê²½ ë¦¬ë“œíƒ€ì„Â·ë¡œë´‡ ê°€ë™ë¥ ì„ ì¶”ì í•˜ë©° ë„¤íŠ¸ì›Œí¬ QoSÂ·ë„ë©”ì¸ ëœë¤í™”Â·ì œë¡œíŠ¸ëŸ¬ìŠ¤íŠ¸Â·íœ´ë¨¼ ì¸ ë” ë£¨í”„ë¡œ ë¦¬ìŠ¤í¬ë¥¼ í†µì œí•˜ë¼. We propose an execution framework that combines Edge Computing, Reinforcement Learning, and Digital Twin to operate robot-based real-time processes with low latency and high flexibility. Begin with local processing (<20ms) of camera/torque sensors in a pilot cell, simulator pre-training and sim-to-real adaptation (ISO 10218/TS 15066 compliance), and Digital Twin-based virtual commissioning and MES/PLC synchronization. First, apply vision pick-and-place, AMR path optimization, and real-time quality inspection; track cycle time, OEE, defect rate, change lead time, and robot utilization rate; and control risks with network QoS, domain randomization, zero-trust, and human-in-the-loop.

Real-time weld pool/bead monitoring, combining optics (machine vision) and acoustics, is a direct means to reduce scrap, rework, and post-inspection costs. A data strategy that divides regions using segmentation and trains with examples of good/defective products, along with an AI-Welder Human-in-the-loop interface (HMI embedded SW and field-friendly alarms), determines the success or failure of practical application. Implementation should begin narrowly with one process and one weld type, expanding with a pilot-to-scale strategy, while simultaneously checking the on-site checklist including camera/lighting and microphone placement, data synchronization, and edge computing.

ì œì¡°ì—…ì—ì„œ ì‚¬ì´ë²„ë³´ì•ˆì€ ì¸í”Œë ˆì´ì…˜Â·ì„±ì¥ ë‹¤ìŒì˜ ìƒìœ„ 3ëŒ€ ë¦¬ìŠ¤í¬ë¡œ, íŠ¹íˆ OT ìì‚° ë³´í˜¸ê°€ ê¸°ìˆ íˆ¬ìì˜ í•µì‹¬ì´ë©° ì œì¡°ì‚¬ì˜ 2/3ê°€ OT ë³´ì•ˆ í”Œë«í¼ì„ ë„ì…í–ˆê³  ë‚˜ë¨¸ì§€ë„ 5ë…„ ë‚´ ê³„íš, 60%+ê°€ 12ê°œì›” ë‚´ AI/ML ê¸°ë°˜ íƒì§€ë¥¼ ì¶”ì§„ ì¤‘ì…ë‹ˆë‹¤. ì‹¤ë¬´ì ìœ¼ë¡œëŠ” OT ìì‚° ì¸ë²¤í† ë¦¬Â·ì·¨ì•½ì  ë² ì´ìŠ¤ë¼ì¸, ë„¤íŠ¸ì›Œí¬ ì¡´/ì»¨ë“€ì‡ ì„¸ë¶„í™”ì™€ ì›ê²©ì ‘ì† í†µì œ, OT ì „ìš© IDSÂ·í”„ë¡œí† ì½œ DPIì™€ AI ì´ìƒì§•í›„ íƒì§€ì˜ SOC ì—°ê³„, ì •ê¸° ë°±ì—…/ë³µêµ¬ í…ŒìŠ¤íŠ¸, ê³µê¸‰ë§ ì ‘ì† ê´€ë¦¬, ì‚¬ê³ ëŒ€ì‘ í”Œë ˆì´ë¶Â·ëª¨ì˜í›ˆë ¨ì„ NIST CSF 2.0Â·ISA/IEC 62443 ê¸°ì¤€ìœ¼ë¡œ ë‹¨ê³„ì ìœ¼ë¡œ ë‚´ì¬í™”í•´ì•¼ í•©ë‹ˆë‹¤. ëœì„¬ì›¨ì–´ê°€ ìƒì‚°ì¤‘ë‹¨ìœ¼ë¡œ ì§ê²°ë˜ëŠ” ë§Œí¼ ìŠ¤ë§ˆíŠ¸íŒ©í† ë¦¬ ì„¤ê³„ ë‹¨ê³„ì—ì„œ ë³´ì•ˆ ìš°ì„  ì›ì¹™ê³¼ KPI(MTTD/MTTR, ê°€ì‹œì„± ì»¤ë²„ë¦¬ì§€, RTO/RPO, ì˜¤íƒë¥ )ë¥¼ ì„¤ì •í•˜ëŠ” ê²ƒì´ íš¨ê³¼ì ì…ë‹ˆë‹¤. In manufacturing, cybersecurity ranks among the top three risks, after inflation and growth. Protecting OT assets is the core of technology investment; two-thirds of manufacturers have adopted OT security platforms, with the remainder planning to do so within five years, and over 60% are pursuing AI/ML-based detection within 12 months. Practically, organizations must gradually internalize OT asset inventory and vulnerability baselines, network zone/conduit segmentation and remote access control, SOC integration of OT-specific IDS, protocol DPI, and AI-based anomaly detection, regular backup/recovery testing, supply chain access management, and incident response playbooks and simulation drills, all based on NIST CSF 2.0 and ISA/IEC 62443 standards. As ransomware directly leads to production disruption, it is effective to establish a security-first principle and KPIs (MTTD/MTTR, visibility coverage, RTO/RPO, false positive rate) at the smart factory design stage.
ìš”ì•½:Summary:

This study quantifies the CapEx, liquidity, and cyber risks caused by the adoption of AI, IoT, and automation through a 5-year financial ratio analysis, and proposes practical measures to realize digital transformation value by connecting finance and operations. It flattens cash flow through phase-by-phase investment from pilot to scale, validated by NPV/Payback/ROCE, and parallel subscription-based (OpEx) models, operates OEE-to-EBIT bridge, CCC reduction, Current/Quick Ratio, Inventory/Fixed Asset Turnover, D/E, and cyber risk indicators as KPIs, and recommends governance including Zero Trust, OT/IT separation, and workforce retraining. It provides a roadmap that increases profitability while controlling risks through prioritizing use cases that directly contribute to operating profit margin and liquidity stress testing.

This paper presents the financial implications of introducing AI/IoT/automation on liquidity, profitability, and leverage, based on a five-year financial ratio analysis of a textile manufacturing company (Precot). Although volatility was observed during the pandemic and initial transition period, recent improvements in operating profit margin and net profit margin, and recovery in liquidity are observed; conversely, weak cash ratio, investment pressure, and cyber risk are identified as key challenges. Practically, the paper recommends phased digital implementation, ROI-based stage-gates, optimization of the CapEx/OpEx mix, digital investment management linked with liquidity and debt management KPIs, and an OT-centric cybersecurity framework. Workforce retraining and enhancement of supply chain digital maturity are emphasized as essential factors for sustainable transformation.

Quantitative analysis of the upstream supply chains for 10 frequently used pharmaceutical ingredients in the Netherlands at the MAHâ€“APIâ€“FPP level revealed vulnerabilities such as geographical concentration, single-source dependency, and insufficient dual sourcing (15% on both sides). The key levers for AI in medical (medical device) manufacturing are predictive maintenance, vision-based quality inspection, demand forecasting and supply chain risk management, and robotic automation. Through data-driven decision-making, these can reduce downtime and scrap, and increase productivity. For implementation, prioritize processes with high defect rates/variability, verify with small-scale PoCs, then expand to line and plant units. Defect feedback should be reflected in real-time through closed-loop control linked with MES and QMS, and the effects should be quantified using KPIs such as FPY, OEE, scrap rate, and MTBF/MTTR. Given the nature of medical manufacturing, its practical application value is high only when compliance with GAMP 5, 21 CFR Part 11, and ISO 13485/14971 is maintained alongside governance that includes HITL, OT security, and SOP updates.

Effects should be quantified using KPIs such as FPY, scrap rate, and OEE, and governance must be established, including compliance with 21 CFR Part 11/ISO 13485, HITL decision-making, change management, and audit trails. AI infrastructure demand has entered a phase of structural growth of 30%+ from 2023 to 2025. Jabil's 2025 AI revenue target of $7.5 billion, an 18% growth rate, and a $500 million facility expansion in the Americas demonstrate a surge in actual manufacturing demand for accelerators, optical/electrical interconnects, and racks within the EMS/ODM sector. From a manufacturing AI transformation perspective, vertical integration of core processes and capacity expansion, S&OP redesign and automation of bottlenecks (precision machining, optical assembly, testing), accelerating NPI/co-design, and deepening and dual-sourcing strategic suppliers alongside alternative BOM strategies are effective. Additionally, in response to reshoring demands, combining US-centric regional production with incentive and local sourcing strategies simultaneously lowers lead time, quality, and regulatory risks.

As the Taiwan risk centered on TSMC is a core vulnerability of semiconductor-dependent manufacturing, quantify exposure up to foundry/process node/packaging and test locations based on BOM, and continuously run digital twin simulations assuming 2-4 times lead time and surging logistics/insurance costs in case of blockade/sanctions. Establish a multi-foundry, multi-node, multi-OSAT (Malaysia, Vietnam, South Korea, U.S., Japan) strategy, utilizing UMC and GlobalFoundries for mature processes, and Samsung, Intel, etc., for advanced processes, and formulate a BCP including a 90-180 day buffer for critical chips, take-or-pay capacity reservation, PDK/IP portability and testbench standardization, rapid PCN approval, and crisis communication. Furthermore, mitigate panic buying and production stoppages by proactively adjusting allocations through early warnings that combine geopolitical, export control, ship AIS, and news signals.

The core of the article is the impact of geopolitical risks surrounding Taiwan and TSMC on the global semiconductor supply chain. From a manufacturing perspective, immediately applicable points include the quantification of Taiwan dependency (BOM, node, packaging, test), lead time and cost impact simulation based on blockade/sanction scenarios, multi-foundry and multi-node design conversion, securing offshore OSAT and advanced packaging capacity, and the establishment of AI-based risk sensing and supply chain digital twins. This document presents the step-by-step authorization procedure for using Generative AI Agents in Oracle Cloud SCM (Custom Role creation, adding intelligent agent-related duty roles, activating privilege groups). The configuration path is separated depending on the presence or absence of the Supply Chain App Administrator role, allowing for rapid application in accordance with the existing RBAC system. Synthetic data resolves data scarcity and security constraints in manufacturing AI Transformation, enabling immediate use for training rare defects in quality inspection, mitigating cold start for predictive maintenance, verifying safety in supply chain scenarios and robotics, and facilitating privacy-preserving collaboration with sensitive data. Upon adoption, its effectiveness can be maximized by selecting physics-based or generative models, applying TSTR (Train on Synthetic, Test on Real) validation, managing re-identification, representativeness, and domain gaps, and tracking ROI through operational metrics such as missed detection rate, downtime, scrap rate, and lead time variability.

This article lists the extensive applications of synthetic data, and from a manufacturing perspective, it highlights the value of quality inspection data augmentation, addressing predictive maintenance cold start, supply chain simulation, robotics safety validation, security-friendly data sharing, and test data generation. Although specific achievements and examples are scarce, it has reference value in that it presents a strategic option for bypassing data constraints and enhancing model robustness in the early stages of manufacturing AI adoption. When implementing, key practical points include considering TSTR validation, domain gap management, privacy governance, and ROI metric design together.

The Korean manufacturing industry must move away from an exclusive reliance on exports and simultaneously pursue domestic demand-oriented digital services (aftermarket, predictive maintenance, subscription-based parts) and export-oriented large-scale projects (shipbuilding, plant engineering) to reduce volatility and transition to a high-skill, high-wage structure. Existing key industries must integrate on-site AI, such as design automation, process scheduling optimization, welding and painting quality prediction, and monitoring of safety and environmental regulation compliance. Furthermore, they must establish a system to protect core data and IP with sovereign AI (on-premise/private cloud, industry-specific foundation models, MLOps governance). Strategies that accelerate execution are effective: a 12-18 month short-term ROI-centric use case roadmap, data standardization and addressing global certification and trade regulations (such as CPTPP), and CoE-based upskilling and partnerships.

Through sovereign AI, while protecting core process data and IP, it is necessary to operate on-premise and industry-specific models, and with CPTPP participation as an opportunity, to prepare for data standardization and the reorganization of global value chains in response to the cumulation of origin and the alleviation of non-tariff barriers. Quantum Ventures Korea's investment strategy, simultaneously targeting semiconductor front-end processes (equipment and metrology) and back-end processes (manufacturing and logistics automation), is highly aligned with the manufacturing AX roadmap that optimizes quality, productivity, and lead time into a single system. From an execution perspective, data-intensive processes such as TGV-based high-density packaging and EUV mask defect inspection should be internalized using AI vision/metrology, and common KPIs for yield, CT, FPY, scrap, and OTD should be designed into the data pipeline connecting front-end and back-end. Furthermore, it is effective to secure Edge AI and data accessibility through joint development with domestic equipment vendors, and to strengthen in-line inspection and tracking along with MES/APS/EMS integration to meet automotive semiconductor standards.

This article provides a warning that the mismatch caused by the overseas outflow and underemployment of STEM and AI talent directly constrains the manufacturing industry's Smart Factory/AX (AI Transformation) implementation capabilities. Manufacturers must redesign their recruitment, development, and collaboration systems to introduce dual recruitment tracks (research-focused PhD vs. field application-oriented data/Operational Technology (OT)-Information Technology (IT) convergence), clarify field data access, experimental environments, and problem definition, and reduce the disconnection between research and business operations using R&D KPIs, TRL, and PoC gates. Furthermore, enhance talent acquisition and engagement through reskilling pipelines (operator â†’ digital technician, facility maintenance â†’ predictive maintenance), factory data-driven industry-academia testbeds, global/hybrid R&D cells, and settlement packages such as visas, relocation, and returnships.

The article presents figures on the outflow of South Korean STEM and AI talent overseas and the R&D job mismatch (e.g., 59.3% of PhDs underemployed, imbalance between jobs and talent supply). This is a structural risk that makes it difficult to secure and retain the data, AI, and automation personnel needed for manufacturing AX. Manufacturers can convert this mismatch into an opportunity through methods such as bifurcated recruitment, on-site reskilling systems, results-oriented industry-academia cooperation, management of global and hybrid talent, and KPI and TRL-based R&D management.

ì‚¬ì¶œÂ·ì„±í˜• ì œì¡°ì— ì ìš© ê°€ëŠ¥í•œ ì‚¬ë¡€ë¡œ, ë¬¼ë¦¬ ë°œí¬ ì„±í˜•ê³¼ ABSâ†’PP ì „í™˜ì„ í†µí•´ ì œí’ˆë‹¹ í”Œë¼ìŠ¤í‹± 900g ë° CO2 14.85kg ì ˆê°ì´ í™•ì¸ë˜ë©°, ì‚¬ì „ CAE(ë³€í˜•Â·ê°•ì„±Â·ìˆ˜ì¶•) ê²€ì¦, ê¸ˆí˜• ê°œì¡° ë²”ìœ„ ì„¤ê³„, ë°œí¬ìœ¨â€“ê¸°ê³„ì  ì„±ëŠ¥ íŠ¸ë ˆì´ë“œì˜¤í”„ ê´€ë¦¬, LCA(ISO 14067) ê¸°ë°˜ ì§€í‘œí™”ê°€ ì‹¤í–‰ í•µì‹¬ì…ë‹ˆë‹¤. ë‚´ì¥í˜• Wiâ€‘Fiì™€ AI ì ˆì•½ ëª¨ë“œë¡œ ìµœëŒ€ 20% ì—ë„ˆì§€ ì ˆê°ì€ â€˜ì—°ê²°+ì•Œê³ ë¦¬ì¦˜â€™ì´ OPEXë¥¼ ë‚®ì¶”ê³  ë¦¬ëª¨íŠ¸ ìµœì í™”Â·ì˜ˆì§€ì •ë¹„ ë“± ì„œë¹„ìŠ¤ ìˆ˜ìµí™”ë¥¼ ì—¬ëŠ” ë°ì´í„° íŒŒì´í”„ë¼ì¸(íŒì›¨ì–´â€“í´ë¼ìš°ë“œ) êµ¬ì¶• í•„ìš”ì„±ì„ ë³´ì—¬ì¤ë‹ˆë‹¤. ë˜í•œ PSM ìµœê³ ë“±ê¸‰Â·ISO 22301 ì¸ì¦ì€ ì„¼ì„œ+AI ê¸°ë°˜ ìœ„í—˜ì„± í‰ê°€ ê³ ë„í™”ì™€ ë‹¤ìš´íƒ€ì„ ì €ê°ì˜ ê±°ë²„ë„ŒìŠ¤ ë°©í–¥ì„ ì‹œì‚¬í•˜ë©°, ë™ë‚¨ì•„ HVAC ìˆ˜ìš” ì¦ê°€ëŠ” ê³µì¥Â·ë¹Œë”© ë¦¬íŠ¸ë¡œí•ì„ í†µí•œ Scope 2 ê°ì¶• ê¸°íšŒë¡œ í•´ì„ë©ë‹ˆë‹¤. As an applicable case in injection and molding manufacturing, a reduction of 900g plastic and 14.85kg CO2 per product is confirmed through physical foam molding and ABS-to-PP conversion. Key to implementation are pre-CAE (deformation, stiffness, shrinkage) verification, mold modification scope design, foaming rate-mechanical performance tradeoff management, and LCA (ISO 14067) based metricization. Up to 20% energy saving with built-in Wi-Fi and AI power-saving mode demonstrates the necessity of establishing a data pipeline (firmware-cloud) where 'connectivity + algorithms' lower OPEX and enable service monetization, such as remote optimization and predictive maintenance. Furthermore, PSM top-tier rating and ISO 22301 certification suggest a governance direction toward advanced sensor- and AI-based risk assessment and downtime reduction. The increased HVAC demand in Southeast Asia is interpreted as an opportunity for Scope 2 reduction through factory and building retrofits.

In the short term, evaluation of integrated control and data platforms and microfactory pilots for on-site repair and spare parts are recommended.

Amid rising tariff baselines, including an average tariff of 18.6% due to the intensifying US-China conflict, a redesign of trade strategies centered on supply chain security and resilience is required, taking into account tariffs of 15% on automobiles and 50% on steel and aluminum. Manufacturers should implement tariff scenario models and digital twin/network optimization (US-Mexico bases, localization of key components) based on TCO-service levels, and establish digitalization of trade compliance (HS AI classification, ROO/origin engine, automated sanction/tariff updates, duty drawback/FTZ) and a system for origin and traceability extending to 2nd and 3rd tier suppliers. In a high-tariff environment for steel and aluminum, it is effective to reflect alternative materials/re-certification and a low-carbon (EAF) / local procurement portfolio in investment plans by linking them with IRA/state government incentives and energy hedging strategies.

This paper quantifies AI risks by process and equipment by applying the formula 'Risk = Hazard Ã— Exposure Ã— Vulnerability' when introducing and operating manufacturing AX, and presents a governance roadmap that prioritizes controlling short-term operational risks such as bias, hallucination, and privacy. It guides the simultaneous management of safety and performance through a phased rollout (safety cases, red teaming, simulation) based on multidisciplinary participation from field workers, quality, EHS, security, etc., and the operation of KPIs (bias/accuracy/hallucination rate, near misses, etc.). Furthermore, by including strengthening data representativeness and exposure reduction (network segmentation, human-in-the-loop), it has high practical applicability.

This text critically examines the AI ontological risk debate, emphasizing that what is needed in manufacturing sites is not 'helpful fear' but systematic risk management and a just transition. It is proposed to apply the Risk = Hazard Ã— Exposure Ã— Vulnerability framework to AI, thereby reducing design, deployment, and operational vulnerabilities respectively, and to prioritize controlling immediate risks such as bias, hallucination, misinformation, and privacy. It is necessary to recognize the limitations of vendor-driven ethics, internalize a safety culture through on-site participatory governance, and promote a just transition through securing global data representation and retraining. At the same time, it is recommended to select AI tasks that simultaneously mitigate multiple risks, such as energy optimization and defect reduction, and to establish verification procedures including phased rollout, red teaming, and safety cases.

Given that reliance on China for the rare earth supply chain is a strategic risk directly linked to smart factory and AI transformation and the stable procurement of core components, it is essential to visualize Nd/Pr/Dy/Tb usage and single-country dependency based on BOM using matrices and HHI, and to continuously manage Cost-at-risk and Days of Cover as KPIs. To proactively optimize delivery time and cost impacts by scenario, simultaneously pursue sourcing diversification from countries like Japan, Australia, the US, and ASEAN; long-term offtake for refining and recycling; strengthening domestic refining and recycling capabilities; and alternative designs such as Dy-reduction and HREE-free magnets, while also utilizing batch tracking, proof of origin, LCA/CFP automation, supply chain digital twin, and risk scoring. By leveraging its strengths in metal refining and utilizing toll refining and industry-academia-research consortiums to establish a recycling system with a target recovery rate of 80%+, Korean manufacturing can simultaneously secure new businesses and DX sustainability.

For Korean companies, there are opportunities for material self-reliance and high value-added creation by utilizing refining technology and industry-academia-research collaboration.

Humanoids can be an alternative for variable and unstructured processes where existing fixed automation was less economical, and for dangerous, repetitive, and heavy (D3) tasks, and are also effective in responding to labor shortages due to an aging population. Manufacturers, predicated on the separation of roles between humans (judgment, precision) and robots (strength, endurance, risk), should introduce human-robot hybrid cells in phases: PoC â†’ Pilot â†’ Diffusion, while concurrently conducting HRC safety training and retraining, and must prepare for MES/ERP integration, digital work instructions, and remote monitoring. Effects should be managed with KPIs such as OEE, cycle time, reduction of accident/musculoskeletal risks, and absentee replacement rate, and areas where equipment is human-centric and where process variations and layout changes are frequent should be selected as priority application targets.

The core of the article is that humanoid robots should complement the labor shortage caused by aging populations, replace dangerous and repetitive tasks, and collaborate complementarily with humans, along with the necessity for retraining. From a manufacturing perspective, a pilot-to-diffusion strategy is effective, prioritizing the application of D3 tasks, concurrently conducting HRC safety and training, and quantifying effects with OEE, cycle time, and safety indicators. Preparing a scalable operating system, considering integration with existing equipment and IT (MES/ERP), is of practical value.

By applying a relationship-based, non-extractive technological philosophy derived from indigenous knowledge to Manufacturing AI Transformation, data sovereignty and reciprocity-centered governance can be established: Through data trusts and federated learning, factory data is kept locally, and exchanges with vendors are controlled via data contracts that specify purpose limitation, retention periods, prohibition of secondary use, and profit sharing. For worker-centric HRI, standardizing granular consent/withdrawal, explainable interfaces, surveillance guidelines (such as edge processing, pseudonymization, and shortening of retention periods), and on-site co-design can increase trust, safety, and acceptance.

SK Group's Icheon Forum redefines AI not as a means of efficiency, but as a growth engine, and proposes a direction to redesign on-site operating systems around AI/DT through 'Operation Improvement 2.0'. From a manufacturing perspective, the key is to portfolio practical AI tasks linked to process, equipment, and SCM KPIs, and to establish data quality, MLOps, and diffusion systems. Additionally, establishing governance linked to management philosophies such as SKMS, and internalizing scenario-based supply chain decision-making that reflects global risks are practical points. Although specific examples are lacking, the strategic direction provides meaningful implications for promoting manufacturing AX.
